Summer Time</a> (<a href="/wiki/UTC%2B01:00" title="UTC+01:00">UTC+1</a>)</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Cornwall</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90r/: &#39;ar&#39; in &#39;war&#39;">\xc9\x94\xcb\x90r</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;w&#39; in &#39;wind&#39;">w</span><span title="/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90/: &#39;au&#39; in &#39;fraud&#39;">\xc9\x94\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span></span>, <wbr />-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;w&#39; in &#39;wind&#39;">w</span><span title="/\xc9\x99l/: &#39;le&#39; in &#39;bottle&#39;">\xc9\x99l</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>: <i lang="kw">Kernow</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[\xcb\x88k\xc9\x9brn\xc9\x94\xca\x8a]</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Ceremonial_counties_of_England" title="Ceremonial counties of England">county</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_West_England" title="South West England">South West England</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>. The county is bordered to the north and west by the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Sea" title="Celtic Sea">Celtic Sea</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> to the south by the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>, and to the east by the county of <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a>, over the <a href="/wiki/River_Tamar" title="River Tamar">River Tamar</a> which forms most of the border between them. Cornwall forms the westernmost part of the <a href="/wiki/South_West_Peninsula" title="South West Peninsula">South West Peninsula</a> of the island of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>. The furthest southwestern point of the island is <a href="/wiki/Land%27s_End" title="Land&#39;s End">Land\'s End</a>; the southernmost point is <a href="/wiki/Lizard_Point,_Cornwall" title="Lizard Point, Cornwall">Lizard Point</a>. Cornwall has a population of 563,600 and covers an area of 3,563&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (1,376&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Town_populations_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Town_populations-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The county has been administered <a href="/wiki/2009_structural_changes_to_local_government_in_England" title="2009 structural changes to local government in England">since 2009</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authorities_of_England" title="Unitary authorities of England">unitary</a> authority, <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">Cornwall Council</a>. The ceremonial county of Cornwall also includes the <a href="/wiki/Isles_of_Scilly" title="Isles of Scilly">Isles of Scilly</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_Isles_of_Scilly" title="Council of the Isles of Scilly">administered separately</a>. The <a href="/wiki/County_town" title="County town">administrative centre</a> of Cornwall, and its only <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">city</a>, is <a href="/wiki/Truro" title="Truro">Truro</a>.\n</p><p>Cornwall is the homeland of the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_people" title="Cornish people">Cornish people</a> and the cultural and ethnic origin of the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_diaspora" title="Cornish diaspora">Cornish diaspora</a>. It retains a distinct <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Cornwall" title="Culture of Cornwall">cultural identity</a> that reflects its unique <a href="/wiki/History_of_Cornwall" title="History of Cornwall">history</a>, and is recognised as one of the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_nations" title="Celtic nations">Celtic nations</a> with a rich cultural heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It was formerly a <a href="/wiki/Britons_(Celtic_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (Celtic people)">Brythonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Dumnonia" title="Dumnonia">kingdom</a> and subsequently a <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Cornwall" title="Duchy of Cornwall">royal duchy</a>. The Cornish <a href="/wiki/Cornish_nationalism" title="Cornish nationalism">nationalist movement</a> contests the present <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_status_of_Cornwall" title="Constitutional status of Cornwall">constitutional status of Cornwall</a> and seeks greater <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a> within the United Kingdom in the form of a devolved legislative <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Assembly" title="Cornish Assembly">Cornish Assembly</a> with powers similar to those in <a href="/wiki/Devolution_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Devolution in the United Kingdom">Wales and Scotland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, Cornish people were granted minority status under the European <a href="/wiki/Framework_Convention_for_the_Protection_of_National_Minorities" title="Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities">Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_minority_status_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_minority_status-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> giving them recognition as a distinct ethnic group.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>First inhabited in the <a href="/wiki/Palaeolithic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palaeolithic">Palaeolithic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> periods, Cornwall continued to be occupied by <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Britain" title="Prehistoric Britain">Neolithic</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Britain" title="Bronze Age Britain">Bronze Age</a> peoples, and later (in the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>) by <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Brythons</a> with strong trade and cultural links to <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Cornwall_and_Devon" title="Mining in Cornwall and Devon">Mining in Cornwall and Devon</a> in the south west of England began in the early <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>.\n</p><p>Few <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman</a> remains have been found in Cornwall, and there is little evidence that the Romans settled or had much military presence there.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">collapse of the Roman Empire</a>, Cornwall was ruled by chieftains of the <a href="/wiki/Cornovii_(Cornwall)" title="Cornovii (Cornwall)">Cornovii</a> who may have included figures regarded as semi-historical or legendary, such as <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Cunomorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcus Cunomorus">King Mark of Cornwall</a> and <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>, evidenced by folklore traditions derived from the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Regum_Britanniae" title="Historia Regum Britanniae">Historia Regum Britanniae</a></i>. The Cornovii division of the <a href="/wiki/Dumnonii" title="Dumnonii">Dumnonii</a> tribe were separated from the Brythons of Wales after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Deorham" title="Battle of Deorham">Battle of Deorham</a> and often came into conflict with the expanding kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>. <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelstan" title="\xc3\x86thelstan">King Athelstan</a> in AD 936 set the boundary between English and Cornish at the high water mark of the eastern bank of the River Tamar.<sup id="cite_ref-Stenton,_F._M._1947_p._337_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stenton,_F._M._1947_p._337-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> From the early Middle Ages, language and culture were shared by Brythons trading across both sides of the Channel, resulting in the corresponding <a href="/wiki/High_mediaeval" class="mw-redirect" title="High mediaeval">high medieval</a> <a href="/wiki/Breton_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Breton people">Breton</a> kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Domnon%C3%A9e" title="Domnon\xc3\xa9e">Domnon\xc3\xa9e</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornouaille" title="Cornouaille">Cornouaille</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christianity</a> common to both areas.\n</p><p>Historically <a href="/wiki/Tin_mining_in_Britain" title="Tin mining in Britain">tin mining</a> was important in the Cornish economy; it was increasingly significant during the High Middle Ages, and expanded greatly during the 19th century, when rich copper mines were also in production. In the mid-19th century, however, the tin and copper mines entered a period of decline. Subsequently, china clay extraction became more important, and metal <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Cornwall_and_Devon" title="Mining in Cornwall and Devon">mining</a> had virtually ended by the 1990s. Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Fishing_in_Cornwall" title="Fishing in Cornwall">fishing</a> (particularly of <a href="/wiki/Pilchards" class="mw-redirect" title="Pilchards">pilchards</a>) and agriculture (notably dairy products and vegetables) were the other important sectors of the economy. Railways led to a growth of tourism in the 20th century; however, <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Cornwall" title="Economy of Cornwall">Cornwall\'s economy</a> struggled after the decline of the mining and fishing industries.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cornwall is noted for its geology and coastal scenery. A large part of the <a href="/wiki/Cornubian_batholith" title="Cornubian batholith">Cornubian batholith</a> is within Cornwall. The north coast has many cliffs where exposed geological formations are studied. The area is noted for its wild <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moorland</a> landscapes, its long and varied coastline, its attractive villages, its many place-names derived from the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish language</a>, and its very mild <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Cornwall#Climate" title="Geography of Cornwall">climate</a>. Extensive stretches of Cornwall\'s coastline, and <a href="/wiki/Bodmin_Moor" title="Bodmin Moor">Bodmin Moor</a>, are protected as <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Area_of_Outstanding_Natural_Beauty" title="Cornwall Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty">an Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_emblems"><span id="Toponymy">Name and emblems</span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name and emblems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg/220px-Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg" width="220" height="316" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg/330px-Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg/440px-Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="685" data-file-height="985" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Cornweallas" shown on an early 19th-century map of "Saxon England" (and Wales) based on the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Land%27s_End,_Cornwall,_England.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Land%27s_End%2C_Cornwall%2C_England.jpg/220px-Land%27s_End%2C_Cornwall%2C_England.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Land%27s_End%2C_Cornwall%2C_England.jpg/330px-Land%27s_End%2C_Cornwall%2C_England.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Land%27s_End%2C_Cornwall%2C_England.jpg/440px-Land%27s_End%2C_Cornwall%2C_England.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Land%27s_End,_Cornwall,_England.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cliffs at Land\'s End</div></div></div>\n<p>The modern <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> name <b>Cornwall</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compound</a> of two ancient <a href="/wiki/Demonyms" class="mw-redirect" title="Demonyms">demonyms</a> coming from two different language groups:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Corn-</b> originates from the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Brythonic</a> tribe, the <i><a href="/wiki/Cornovii_(Cornwall)" title="Cornovii (Cornwall)">Cornovii</a></i> ("peninsula people"). The <a href="/wiki/Celtic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic language">Celtic word</a> "<i>kernou</i>" ("horn" or "headland") is cognate with the English word "horn" (both deriving from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> *ker-).<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>-wall</b> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> exonym <i><a href="/wiki/Walhaz" title="Walhaz">w(e)alh</a></i>, meaning "foreigner" or "Roman" (i.e. a <a href="/wiki/Welshman" class="mw-redirect" title="Welshman">Welshman</a>).</li></ul>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish language</a>, Cornwall is known as <b>Kernow</b> which stems from a similar linguistic background.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flag">Flag</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Flag">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Saint_Piran%27s_Flag" title="Saint Piran&#39;s Flag">Saint Piran\'s Flag</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pamiatka_z_Kornwalii.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Pamiatka_z_Kornwalii.jpg/220px-Pamiatka_z_Kornwalii.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Pamiatka_z_Kornwalii.jpg/330px-Pamiatka_z_Kornwalii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Pamiatka_z_Kornwalii.jpg/440px-Pamiatka_z_Kornwalii.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="479" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pamiatka_z_Kornwalii.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Souvenir flags outside a Cornish caf\xc3\xa9</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Saint_Piran" title="Saint Piran">Saint Piran</a>\'s Flag is the national flag and ancient banner of Cornwall,<sup id="cite_ref-auto_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rendle_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rendle-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fotw_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fotw-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and an emblem of the Cornish people. It is regarded as the county flag by <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">Cornwall Council</a>. The banner of Saint Piran is a white cross on a black background (in terms of <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldry</a> \'sable, a cross argent\'). According to legend Saint Piran adopted these colours from seeing the white tin in the black coals and ashes during his discovery of tin.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Cornish flag is an exact reverse of the former <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Breton</a> national flag (black cross) and is known by the same name "Kroaz Du".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heraldry">Heraldry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Heraldry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_heraldry" title="Cornish heraldry">Cornish heraldry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornish_corporate_heraldry" title="Cornish corporate heraldry">Cornish corporate heraldry</a></div>\n<p>In later times, Cornwall was known to the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a> as "West Wales" to distinguish it from "North Wales" (the modern nation of <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The name appears in the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i> in 891 as <i>On Corn walum</i>. In the <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a> it was referred to as <i>Cornualia</i> and in c. 1198 as <i>Cornwal</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-VW_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VW-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> Other names for the county include a <a href="/wiki/Latinisation_of_names" title="Latinisation of names">latinisation</a> of the name as <i>Cornubia</i> (first appears in a mid-9th-century deed purporting to be a copy of one dating from c. 705), and as <i>Cornugallia</i> in 1086.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_geography">Physical geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Physical geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Cornwall" title="Geography of Cornwall">Geography of Cornwall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Cornwall" title="Geology of Cornwall">Geology of Cornwall</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cw2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Cw2.jpg/220px-Cw2.jpg" width="220" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Cw2.jpg/330px-Cw2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7e/Cw2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="373" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cw2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite image of Cornwall</div></div></div>\n<p>Cornwall forms the tip of the south-west peninsula of the island of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, and is therefore exposed to the full force of the <a href="/wiki/Prevailing_winds" title="Prevailing winds">prevailing winds</a> that blow in from the Atlantic Ocean. The coastline is composed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Resistance_(geology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Resistance (geology)">resistant</a> rocks that give rise in many places to tall cliffs. Cornwall has a border with only one other county, <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a>, which is formed almost entirely by the <a href="/wiki/River_Tamar" title="River Tamar">River Tamar</a>, and the remainder (to the north) by the <a href="/wiki/Marsland_Valley" title="Marsland Valley">Marsland Valley</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_areas">Coastal areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Coastal areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The north and south coasts have different characteristics. The north coast on the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Sea" title="Celtic Sea">Celtic Sea</a>, part of the Atlantic Ocean, is more exposed and therefore has a wilder nature. The prosaically named <i>High Cliff</i>, between <a href="/wiki/Boscastle" title="Boscastle">Boscastle</a> and <a href="/wiki/St_Gennys" title="St Gennys">St Gennys</a>, is the highest sheer-drop cliff in Cornwall at 223 metres (732&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> However, there are also many extensive stretches of fine golden sand which form the beaches that are so important to the tourist industry, such as those at <a href="/wiki/Bude" title="Bude">Bude</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polzeath" title="Polzeath">Polzeath</a>, <a href="/wiki/Watergate_Bay" title="Watergate Bay">Watergate Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perranporth" title="Perranporth">Perranporth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porthtowan" title="Porthtowan">Porthtowan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fistral_Beach" title="Fistral Beach">Fistral Beach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newquay" title="Newquay">Newquay</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Agnes,_Cornwall" title="St Agnes, Cornwall">St Agnes</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Ives,_Cornwall" title="St Ives, Cornwall">St Ives</a>, and on the south coast <a href="/wiki/Gyllyngvase" title="Gyllyngvase">Gyllyngvase</a> beach in <a href="/wiki/Falmouth,_Cornwall" title="Falmouth, Cornwall">Falmouth</a> and the large beach at <a href="/wiki/Praa_Sands" title="Praa Sands">Praa Sands</a> further to the south west. There are two river estuaries on the north coast: <a href="/wiki/Hayle_Estuary" title="Hayle Estuary">Hayle Estuary</a> and the estuary of the <a href="/wiki/River_Camel" title="River Camel">River Camel</a>, which provides <a href="/wiki/Padstow" title="Padstow">Padstow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock,_Cornwall" title="Rock, Cornwall">Rock</a> with a safe harbour. The seaside town of <a href="/wiki/Newlyn" title="Newlyn">Newlyn</a> is a popular holiday destination, as it is one of the last remaining traditional Cornish fishing ports, with views reaching over Mount\'s Bay.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St-Michael_Mount.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/St-Michael_Mount.jpg/220px-St-Michael_Mount.jpg" width="220" height="86" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/St-Michael_Mount.jpg/330px-St-Michael_Mount.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/St-Michael_Mount.jpg/440px-St-Michael_Mount.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1174" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St-Michael_Mount.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St_Michael%27s_Mount" title="St Michael&#39;s Mount">St Michael\'s Mount</a> in Marazion</div></div></div>\n<p>The south coast, dubbed the "Cornish Riviera", is more sheltered and there are several broad estuaries offering safe anchorages, such as at Falmouth and <a href="/wiki/Fowey" title="Fowey">Fowey</a>. Beaches on the south coast usually consist of coarser sand and shingle, interspersed with rocky sections of <a href="/wiki/Wave-cut_platform" title="Wave-cut platform">wave-cut platform</a>. Also on the south coast, the picturesque fishing village of <a href="/wiki/Polperro" title="Polperro">Polperro</a>, at the mouth of the Pol River, and the fishing port of <a href="/wiki/Looe" title="Looe">Looe</a> on the <a href="/wiki/River_Looe" title="River Looe">River Looe</a> are both popular with tourists.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inland_areas">Inland areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Inland areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The interior of the county consists of a roughly east\xe2\x80\x93west spine of infertile and exposed upland, with a series of <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> intrusions, such as <a href="/wiki/Bodmin_Moor" title="Bodmin Moor">Bodmin Moor</a>, which contains the highest land within Cornwall. From east to west, and with approximately descending altitude, these are Bodmin Moor, <a href="/wiki/Hensbarrow" title="Hensbarrow">Hensbarrow</a> north of <a href="/wiki/St_Austell" title="St Austell">St Austell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carnmenellis" title="Carnmenellis">Carnmenellis</a> to the south of <a href="/wiki/Camborne" title="Camborne">Camborne</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Penwith" title="Penwith">Penwith</a> or <a href="/wiki/Land%27s_End" title="Land&#39;s End">Land\'s End</a> peninsula. These intrusions are the central part of the granite outcrops that form the exposed parts of the <a href="/wiki/Cornubian_batholith" title="Cornubian batholith">Cornubian batholith</a> of south-west Britain, which also includes <a href="/wiki/Dartmoor" title="Dartmoor">Dartmoor</a> to the east in Devon and the <a href="/wiki/Isles_of_Scilly" title="Isles of Scilly">Isles of Scilly</a> to the west, the latter now being partially submerged.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Porthcurno_beach.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Porthcurno_beach.jpg/220px-Porthcurno_beach.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Porthcurno_beach.jpg/330px-Porthcurno_beach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Porthcurno_beach.jpg/440px-Porthcurno_beach.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Porthcurno_beach.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cornwall is known for its beaches (Porthcurno beach illustrated) and rugged coastline</div></div></div>\n<p>The intrusion of the granite into the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary" class="mw-redirect" title="Sedimentary">sedimentary</a> rocks gave rise to extensive <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">metamorphism</a> and mineralisation, and this led to Cornwall being one of the most important mining areas in Europe until the early 20th century. It is thought <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> was mined here as early as the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, and copper, lead, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> and silver have all been <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Cornwall" class="mw-redirect" title="Mining in Cornwall">mined in Cornwall</a>. Alteration of the granite also gave rise to extensive deposits of <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">China Clay</a>, especially in the area to the north of St Austell, and the extraction of this remains an important industry.\n</p><p>The uplands are surrounded by more fertile, mainly <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pastoral</a> farmland. Near the south coast, deep wooded valleys provide sheltered conditions for flora that like shade and a moist, mild climate. These areas lie mainly on <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slate" title="Slate">slate</a>. The north east of Cornwall lies on <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> rocks known as the <a href="/wiki/Culm_Measures" title="Culm Measures">Culm Measures</a>. In places these have been subjected to severe folding, as can be seen on the north coast near <a href="/wiki/Crackington_Haven" title="Crackington Haven">Crackington Haven</a> and in several other locations.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lizard_Peninsula">Lizard Peninsula</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Lizard Peninsula">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lizard_complex" title="Lizard complex">Lizard complex</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Lizard,_Cornwall" class="mw-redirect" title="Geology of Lizard, Cornwall">geology of the Lizard peninsula</a> is unusual, in that it is mainland Britain\'s only example of an <a href="/wiki/Ophiolite" title="Ophiolite">ophiolite</a>, a section of oceanic crust now found on land.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> Much of the peninsula consists of the dark green and red <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Serpentinite" title="Serpentinite">serpentinite</a>, which forms spectacular cliffs, notably at <a href="/wiki/Kynance_Cove" title="Kynance Cove">Kynance Cove</a>, and carved and polished serpentine ornaments are sold in local gift shops. This <a href="/wiki/Ultramafic_rock" title="Ultramafic rock">ultramafic rock</a> also forms a very infertile soil which covers the flat and marshy heaths of the interior of the peninsula. This is home to rare plants, such as the <a href="/wiki/Erica_vagans" title="Erica vagans">Cornish Heath</a>, which has been adopted as the <a href="/wiki/County_flower" class="mw-redirect" title="County flower">county flower</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hills_and_high_points">Hills and high points</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hills and high points">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_hills_of_Cornwall" title="List of hills of Cornwall">List of hills of Cornwall</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Settlements_and_transport">Settlements and transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Settlements and transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_settlements_in_Cornwall_by_population" title="List of settlements in Cornwall by population">List of settlements in Cornwall by population</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Cornwall" title="Transport in Cornwall">Transport in Cornwall</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Truro_stmarysst.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Truro_stmarysst.jpg/220px-Truro_stmarysst.jpg" width="220" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Truro_stmarysst.jpg/330px-Truro_stmarysst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Truro_stmarysst.jpg/440px-Truro_stmarysst.jpg 2x" data-file-width="529" data-file-height="711" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Truro_stmarysst.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Truro" title="Truro">Truro</a>, Cornwall\'s administrative centre and only city.</div></div></div>\n<p>Cornwall\'s only city, and the home of the <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">council headquarters</a>, is <a href="/wiki/Truro" title="Truro">Truro</a>. Nearby <a href="/wiki/Falmouth,_Cornwall" title="Falmouth, Cornwall">Falmouth</a> is notable as a port. <a href="/wiki/St_Just_in_Penwith" title="St Just in Penwith">St Just in Penwith</a> is the westernmost town in England, though the same claim has been made for <a href="/wiki/Penzance" title="Penzance">Penzance</a>, which is larger. <a href="/wiki/St_Ives,_Cornwall" title="St Ives, Cornwall">St Ives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Padstow" title="Padstow">Padstow</a> are today small vessel ports with a major tourism and leisure sector in their economies. <a href="/wiki/Newquay" title="Newquay">Newquay</a> on the north coast is another major urban settlement which is famous for its beaches and is a popular surfing destination, as is <a href="/wiki/Bude" title="Bude">Bude</a> further north, but Newquay is now also becoming important for its aviation-related industries. <a href="/wiki/St_Austell" title="St Austell">St Austell</a> is the county\'s largest town and more populous than the capital Truro; it was the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Kaolin" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaolin">china clay</a> industry in Cornwall. <a href="/wiki/Redruth" title="Redruth">Redruth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camborne" title="Camborne">Camborne</a> form the largest urban area in Cornwall, and both towns were significant as centres of the global tin mining industry in the 19th century; nearby copper mines were also very productive during that period.\n</p><p>Cornwall borders the county of <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> at the River Tamar. Major roads between Cornwall and the rest of Great Britain are the <a href="/wiki/A38_road" title="A38 road">A38</a> which crosses the Tamar at <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Tamar_Bridge" title="Tamar Bridge">Tamar Bridge</a> and the town of <a href="/wiki/Saltash" title="Saltash">Saltash</a>, the <a href="/wiki/A39_road" title="A39 road">A39 road</a> (Atlantic Highway) from <a href="/wiki/Barnstaple" title="Barnstaple">Barnstaple</a>, passing through <a href="/wiki/North_Cornwall" title="North Cornwall">North Cornwall</a> to end in Falmouth, and the <a href="/wiki/A30_road" title="A30 road">A30</a> which crosses the border south of <a href="/wiki/Launceston,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Launceston, England">Launceston</a>, crosses Bodmin Moor and connects Bodmin and Truro. <a href="/wiki/Torpoint_Ferry" title="Torpoint Ferry">Torpoint Ferry</a> links Plymouth with <a href="/wiki/Torpoint" title="Torpoint">Torpoint</a> on the opposite side of the <a href="/wiki/Hamoaze" title="Hamoaze">Hamoaze</a>. A rail bridge, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Albert_Bridge" title="Royal Albert Bridge">Royal Albert Bridge</a> built by <a href="/wiki/Isambard_Kingdom_Brunel" title="Isambard Kingdom Brunel">Isambard Kingdom Brunel</a> (1859), provides the only other land transport link. The city of Plymouth, a large urban centre in south west Devon, is an important location for services such as hospitals, department stores, road and rail transport, and cultural venues, particularly for people living in east Cornwall.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a>, across the Bristol Channel, have at some times in the past been connected to Cornwall by ferry, but these do not operate now.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Isles_of_Scilly" title="Isles of Scilly">Isles of Scilly</a> are served by ferry (from Penzance) and by aeroplane, having its own airport: <a href="/wiki/St_Mary%27s_Airport_(Isles_of_Scilly)" class="mw-redirect" title="St Mary&#39;s Airport (Isles of Scilly)">St Mary\'s Airport</a>. There are regular flights between St Mary\'s and <a href="/wiki/Land%27s_End_Airport" title="Land&#39;s End Airport">Land\'s End Airport</a>, near St Just, and <a href="/wiki/Newquay_Airport" title="Newquay Airport">Newquay Airport</a>; during the summer season, a service is also provided between St Mary\'s and <a href="/wiki/Exeter_Airport" title="Exeter Airport">Exeter Airport</a>, in Devon.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora_and_fauna">Flora and fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Flora and fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Flora_and_fauna_of_Cornwall" title="Flora and fauna of Cornwall">Flora and fauna of Cornwall</a></div>\n<p>Cornwall has varied habitats including terrestrial and marine ecosystems. One noted species in decline locally is the <a href="/wiki/Reindeer_lichen" class="mw-redirect" title="Reindeer lichen">Reindeer lichen</a>, which species has been made a priority for protection under the national UK <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_Action_Plan" class="mw-redirect" title="Biodiversity Action Plan">Biodiversity Action Plan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Choughfly816.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Choughfly816.jpg/220px-Choughfly816.jpg" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Choughfly816.jpg/330px-Choughfly816.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Choughfly816.jpg 2x" data-file-width="336" data-file-height="308" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Choughfly816.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_chough" title="Red-billed chough">red-billed chough</a> (<i>Pyrrhocorax pyrrhocorax pyrrhocorax</i>), once commonly seen throughout Cornwall, experienced a severe decline in its population in the 20th century.</div></div></div>\n<p>Botanists divide Cornwall and Scilly into two vice-counties: West (1) and East (2). The standard flora is by <a href="/wiki/F._H._Davey" class="mw-redirect" title="F. H. Davey">F. H. Davey</a> <i>Flora of Cornwall</i> (1909). Davey was assisted by <a href="/wiki/A._O._Hume" class="mw-redirect" title="A. O. Hume">A. O. Hume</a> and he thanks Hume, his companion on excursions in Cornwall and Devon, and for help in the compilation of that Flora, publication of which was financed by him.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Cornwall#Climate" title="Geography of Cornwall">Geography of Cornwall \xc2\xa7&#160;Climate</a></div>\n<p>Cornwall has a <a href="/wiki/Temperate" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperate">temperate</a> <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">Oceanic climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification">K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification</a>: Cfb), with mild winters and cool summers. Cornwall and has the mildest and sunniest climate in the United Kingdom, as a result of its oceanic setting and the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The average annual temperature in Cornwall ranges from 11.6&#160;\xc2\xb0C (52.9&#160;\xc2\xb0F) on the <a href="/wiki/Isles_of_Scilly" title="Isles of Scilly">Isles of Scilly</a> to 9.8&#160;\xc2\xb0C (49.6&#160;\xc2\xb0F) in the central uplands. Winters are among the warmest in the country due to the moderating effects of the warm ocean currents, and frost and snow are very rare at the coast and in the central upland areas. Summers are, however, not as warm as in other parts of southern England.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The surrounding sea and its southwesterly position mean that Cornwall\'s weather can be relatively changeable.\n</p><p>Cornwall is one of the sunniest areas in the UK. It has more than 1,541 hours of sunshine per year, with the highest average of 7.6 hours of sunshine per day in July.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The moist, mild air coming from the southwest brings higher amounts of rainfall than in eastern Great Britain, at 1,051 to 1,290&#160;mm (41.4 to 50.8&#160;in) per year. However, this is not as much as in more northern areas of the west coast.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The Isles of Scilly, for example, where there are on average fewer than two days of air frost per year, is the only area in the UK to be in the <a href="/wiki/Hardiness_zone" title="Hardiness zone">Hardiness zone</a> 10. The islands have, on average, less than one day of air temperature exceeding 30&#160;\xc2\xb0C per year and are in the AHS Heat Zone 1. Extreme temperatures in Cornwall are particularly rare; however, extreme weather in the form of storms and floods is common.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Cornwall" title="Culture of Cornwall">Culture of Cornwall</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Cornwall" title="Languages of Cornwall">Languages of Cornwall</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cornish_language">Cornish language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cornish language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish language</a></div>\n<p>English is the main language used in Cornwall, although the revived <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish language</a> is used, and is spoken fluently by a small minority of people. Most street names and some road signs are written bilingually in Cornish and English.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg/220px-Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg/330px-Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="364" data-file-height="305" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Welcome_sign" title="Welcome sign">welcome sign</a> to <a href="/wiki/Penzance" title="Penzance">Penzance</a>, in the English and <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a> languages</div></div></div>\n<p>The Cornish language is a language from the Brythonic branch of the Celtic language family, closely related to the other <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic languages</a> of <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a>, and less so to the <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic languages</a> of <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scots_Gaelic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Scots Gaelic language">Scots Gaelic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a>. The language continued to function visibly as a community language in parts of Cornwall until the late 18th century, and it was claimed in 2011 that the last native speaker did not die until 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There has been a revival of the language since <a href="/wiki/Henry_Jenner" title="Henry Jenner">Henry Jenner</a>\'s <i>Handbook of the Cornish Language</i> was published in 1904. A study in 2000 suggested that there were around 300 people who spoke Cornish fluently.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Cornish, however, had no legal status in the UK until 2002. Nevertheless, the language is taught in about twelve primary schools, and occasionally used in religious and civic ceremonies.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In 2002 Cornish was officially recognised as a UK minority language<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and in 2005 it received limited Government funding.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Standard_Written_Form" title="Standard Written Form">Standard Written Form</a> was agreed in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several Cornish mining words are used in English language mining terminology, such as <a href="/wiki/Costean" title="Costean">costean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gossan" title="Gossan">gossan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gunnies" title="Gunnies">gunnies</a>, kibbal,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/keeve" class="extiw" title="wikt:keeve">kieve</a><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Vug" title="Vug">vug</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 2010\xe2\x80\x9315 <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a>, four Cornish MPs, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_George_(politician)" title="Andrew George (politician)">Andrew George</a>, MP for <a href="/wiki/St_Ives_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="St Ives (UK Parliament constituency)">St Ives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dan_Rogerson" title="Dan Rogerson">Dan Rogerson</a>, MP for <a href="/wiki/North_Cornwall_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="North Cornwall (UK Parliament constituency)">North Cornwall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Gilbert" title="Steve Gilbert">Steve Gilbert</a>, MP for <a href="/wiki/St_Austell_and_Newquay_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="St Austell and Newquay (UK Parliament constituency)">St Austell and Newquay</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Newton" title="Sarah Newton">Sarah Newton</a>, MP for <a href="/wiki/Truro_and_Falmouth_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Truro and Falmouth (UK Parliament constituency)">Truro and Falmouth</a> repeated their Parliamentary oaths in Cornish.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="English_dialect">English dialect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: English dialect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Cornish" title="Anglo-Cornish">Anglo-Cornish</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Country_Dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="West Country Dialects">West Country Dialects</a></div>\n<p>The Cornish language and culture influenced the emergence of particular pronunciations and grammar not used elsewhere in England. The Cornish dialect is spoken to varying degrees; however, someone speaking full <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Cornish" title="Anglo-Cornish">Anglo-Cornish</a> may be practically unintelligible to one not accustomed it. Cornish dialect has generally declined, in most places it is now little more than a regional accent and grammatical differences have been eroded over time.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">Arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Media_in_Cornwall" title="Media in Cornwall">Media in Cornwall</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tate_gallery_St-Ives.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Tate_gallery_St-Ives.jpg/220px-Tate_gallery_St-Ives.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Tate_gallery_St-Ives.jpg/330px-Tate_gallery_St-Ives.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Tate_gallery_St-Ives.jpg/440px-Tate_gallery_St-Ives.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1068" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tate_gallery_St-Ives.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tate Gallery at <a href="/wiki/St_Ives,_Cornwall" title="St Ives, Cornwall">St Ives</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sphere_With_Inner_Form.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Sphere_With_Inner_Form.jpg/220px-Sphere_With_Inner_Form.jpg" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Sphere_With_Inner_Form.jpg/330px-Sphere_With_Inner_Form.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Sphere_With_Inner_Form.jpg/440px-Sphere_With_Inner_Form.jpg 2x" data-file-width="819" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sphere_With_Inner_Form.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artwork in the <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Hepworth" title="Barbara Hepworth">Barbara Hepworth</a> Museum in St Ives</div></div></div>\n<p>Since the 19th century, Cornwall, with its unspoilt maritime scenery and strong light, has sustained a vibrant visual art scene of international renown. Artistic activity within Cornwall was initially centred on the art-colony of <a href="/wiki/Newlyn" title="Newlyn">Newlyn</a>, most active at the turn of the 20th century. This <a href="/wiki/Newlyn_School" title="Newlyn School">Newlyn School</a> is associated with the names of <a href="/wiki/Stanhope_Forbes" title="Stanhope Forbes">Stanhope Forbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Forbes_(artist)" title="Elizabeth Forbes (artist)">Elizabeth Forbes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norman_Garstin" title="Norman Garstin">Norman Garstin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lamorna_Birch" title="Lamorna Birch">Lamorna Birch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Modernist writers such as <a href="/wiki/D._H._Lawrence" title="D. H. Lawrence">D. H. Lawrence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a> lived in Cornwall between the wars,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ben_Nicholson" title="Ben Nicholson">Ben Nicholson</a>, the painter, having visited in the 1920s came to live in St Ives with his then wife, the sculptor <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Hepworth" title="Barbara Hepworth">Barbara Hepworth</a>, at the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> They were later joined by the Russian emigrant <a href="/wiki/Naum_Gabo" title="Naum Gabo">Naum Gabo</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> and other artists. These included <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lanyon" title="Peter Lanyon">Peter Lanyon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terry_Frost" title="Terry Frost">Terry Frost</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Heron" title="Patrick Heron">Patrick Heron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Wynter" title="Bryan Wynter">Bryan Wynter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Hilton" title="Roger Hilton">Roger Hilton</a>. St Ives also houses the Leach Pottery, where <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Leach" title="Bernard Leach">Bernard Leach</a>, and his followers championed Japanese inspired studio pottery.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Much of this modernist work can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Tate_St_Ives" title="Tate St Ives">Tate St Ives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The Newlyn Society and <a href="/wiki/Penwith_Society_of_Arts" title="Penwith Society of Arts">Penwith Society of Arts</a> continue to be active, and contemporary visual art is documented in a dedicated online journal.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Cornwall" title="Music of Cornwall">Music of Cornwall</a></div>\n<p>Cornwall has a full and vibrant <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk music</a> tradition which has survived into the present and is well known for its unusual folk survivals such as <a href="/wiki/Mummers_Play" class="mw-redirect" title="Mummers Play">Mummers Plays</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Furry_Dance" title="Furry Dance">Furry Dance</a> in <a href="/wiki/Helston" title="Helston">Helston</a> played by the famous <a href="/wiki/Helston_Town_Band" title="Helston Town Band">Helston Town Band</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Obby_Oss" class="mw-redirect" title="Obby Oss">Obby Oss</a> in <a href="/wiki/Padstow" title="Padstow">Padstow</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Newlyn" title="Newlyn">Newlyn</a> is home to a food and music festival<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> which hosts live music, cooking demonstrations, and displays of locally caught fish.\n</p><p>As in other former mining districts of Britain, male voice choirs and <a href="/wiki/Brass_band_(British_style)" class="mw-redirect" title="Brass band (British style)">Brass Bands</a>, e.g. <i>Brass on the Grass</i> concerts during the summer at <a href="/wiki/Constantine,_Kerrier" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine, Kerrier">Constantine</a>, are still very popular in Cornwall: Cornwall also has around 40 brass bands, including the six-times National Champions of Great Britain, Camborne Youth Band, and the bands of <a href="/wiki/Lanner,_Cornwall" title="Lanner, Cornwall">Lanner</a> and St Dennis.\n</p><p>Cornish players are regular participants in inter-Celtic festivals, and Cornwall itself has several lively inter-Celtic festivals such as <a href="/wiki/Perranporth" title="Perranporth">Perranporth</a>\'s Lowender Peran folk festival.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On a more modern note, contemporary musician <a href="/wiki/Aphex_Twin" title="Aphex Twin">Richard D. James</a> (also known as Aphex Twin) grew up in Cornwall, as did <a href="/wiki/Luke_Vibert" title="Luke Vibert">Luke Vibert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alex_Parks" title="Alex Parks">Alex Parks</a>, winner of <a href="/wiki/Fame_Academy" title="Fame Academy">Fame Academy</a> 2003. <a href="/wiki/Roger_Taylor_(Queen_drummer)" title="Roger Taylor (Queen drummer)">Roger Taylor</a>, the drummer from the band <a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">Queen</a> was also raised in the county, and currently lives not far from <a href="/wiki/Falmouth,_Cornwall" title="Falmouth, Cornwall">Falmouth</a>. The American singer-songwriter <a href="/wiki/Tori_Amos" title="Tori Amos">Tori Amos</a> now resides predominantly in North Cornwall not far from Bude with her family.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Lute" title="Lute">lutenist</a>, lutarist, composer and festival director <a href="/wiki/Ben_Salfield" title="Ben Salfield">Ben Salfield</a> lives in <a href="/wiki/Truro" title="Truro">Truro</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Cornwall\'s rich heritage and dramatic landscape have inspired numerous writers.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiction">Fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Poldark" title="Poldark">Poldark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Winston_Graham" title="Winston Graham">Winston Graham</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Quiller-Couch" title="Arthur Quiller-Couch">Sir Arthur Quiller-Couch</a>, author of many novels and works of literary criticism, lived in Fowey: his novels are mainly set in Cornwall. <a href="/wiki/Daphne_du_Maurier" title="Daphne du Maurier">Daphne du Maurier</a> lived at <a href="/wiki/Menabilly" title="Menabilly">Menabilly</a> near Fowey and many of her novels had Cornish settings, including <i><a href="/wiki/Rebecca_(novel)" title="Rebecca (novel)">Rebecca</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Jamaica_Inn_(novel)" title="Jamaica Inn (novel)">Jamaica Inn</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Frenchman%27s_Creek_(novel)" title="Frenchman&#39;s Creek (novel)">Frenchman\'s Creek</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/My_Cousin_Rachel" title="My Cousin Rachel">My Cousin Rachel</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_House_on_the_Strand" title="The House on the Strand">The House on the Strand</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> She is also noted for writing <i>Vanishing Cornwall</i>. Cornwall provided the inspiration for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birds_(story)" title="The Birds (story)">The Birds</a></i>, one of her terrifying series of short stories, made famous as a film by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RemainsofTintagel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/RemainsofTintagel.jpg/220px-RemainsofTintagel.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/RemainsofTintagel.jpg/330px-RemainsofTintagel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/RemainsofTintagel.jpg/440px-RemainsofTintagel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2032" data-file-height="1524" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RemainsofTintagel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Remains of <a href="/wiki/Tintagel_Castle" title="Tintagel Castle">Tintagel Castle</a>, reputedly <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>\'s birthplace</div></div></div>\n<p>Medieval Cornwall is the setting of the trilogy by <a href="/wiki/Monica_Furlong" title="Monica Furlong">Monica Furlong</a>, <i>Wise Child</i>, <i>Juniper</i>, and <i>Colman</i>, as well as part of Charles Kingsley\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Hereward_the_Wake_(novel)" title="Hereward the Wake (novel)">Hereward the Wake</a></i>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Conan_Doyle" class="mw-redirect" title="Conan Doyle">Conan Doyle</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventure_of_the_Devil%27s_Foot" title="The Adventure of the Devil&#39;s Foot">The Adventure of the Devil\'s Foot</a></i> featuring <a href="/wiki/Sherlock_Holmes" title="Sherlock Holmes">Sherlock Holmes</a> is set in Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Winston_Graham" title="Winston Graham">Winston Graham</a>\'s series <i><a href="/wiki/Poldark" title="Poldark">Poldark</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Kate_Tremayne" title="Kate Tremayne">Kate Tremayne</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Adam_Loveday" title="Adam Loveday">Adam Loveday</a> series, <a href="/wiki/Susan_Cooper" title="Susan Cooper">Susan Cooper</a>\'s novels <i><a href="/wiki/Over_Sea,_Under_Stone" title="Over Sea, Under Stone">Over Sea, Under Stone</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Greenwitch</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Wesley" title="Mary Wesley">Mary Wesley</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Camomile_Lawn" title="The Camomile Lawn">The Camomile Lawn</a></i> are all set in Cornwall. Writing under the pseudonym of Alexander Kent, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Reeman" title="Douglas Reeman">Douglas Reeman</a> sets parts of his <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bolitho" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Bolitho">Richard Bolitho</a> and Adam Bolitho series in the Cornwall of the late 18th and the early 19th centuries, particularly in Falmouth.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hammond_Innes" title="Hammond Innes">Hammond Innes</a>\'s novel, <i>The Killer Mine</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Lint" title="Charles de Lint">Charles de Lint</a>\'s novel <i>The Little Country</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> and Chapters 24 and 25 of <a href="/wiki/J._K._Rowling" title="J. K. Rowling">J. K. Rowling</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Deathly_Hallows" title="Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows">Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows</a></i> take place in Cornwall (the Harry Potter story at Shell Cottage, which is on the beach outside the fictional village of Tinworth in Cornwall).<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>David Cornwell, who writes espionage novels under the name <a href="/wiki/John_le_Carr%C3%A9" title="John le Carr\xc3\xa9">John le Carr\xc3\xa9</a>, lives and writes in Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize-winning</a> novelist <a href="/wiki/William_Golding" title="William Golding">William Golding</a> was born in <a href="/wiki/St_Columb_Minor" title="St Columb Minor">St Columb Minor</a> in 1911, and returned to live near Truro from 1985 until his death in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/D._H._Lawrence" title="D. H. Lawrence">D. H. Lawrence</a> spent a short time living in Cornwall. <a href="/wiki/Rosamunde_Pilcher" title="Rosamunde Pilcher">Rosamunde Pilcher</a> grew up in Cornwall, and several of her books take place there.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry">Poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ForTheFallenPlaqueCornwall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/ForTheFallenPlaqueCornwall.jpg/220px-ForTheFallenPlaqueCornwall.jpg" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/ForTheFallenPlaqueCornwall.jpg/330px-ForTheFallenPlaqueCornwall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/ForTheFallenPlaqueCornwall.jpg/440px-ForTheFallenPlaqueCornwall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="439" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ForTheFallenPlaqueCornwall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\'FOR THE FALLEN\' plaque with <a href="/wiki/The_Rumps" title="The Rumps">the Rumps</a> promontory beyond</div></div></div>\n<p>The late <a href="/wiki/Poet_Laureate" class="mw-redirect" title="Poet Laureate">Poet Laureate</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Betjeman" title="John Betjeman">Sir John Betjeman</a> was famously fond of Cornwall and it featured prominently in his poetry. He is buried in the churchyard at <a href="/wiki/St_Enodoc%27s_Church,_Trebetherick" title="St Enodoc&#39;s Church, Trebetherick">St Enodoc\'s Church, Trebetherick</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Charles_Causley" title="Charles Causley">Charles Causley</a>, the poet, was born in Launceston and is perhaps the best known of Cornish poets. <a href="/wiki/Jack_Clemo" title="Jack Clemo">Jack Clemo</a> and the scholar <a href="/wiki/A._L._Rowse" title="A. L. Rowse">A. L. Rowse</a> were also notable Cornishmen known for their poetry; The Rev. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stephen_Hawker" title="Robert Stephen Hawker">R. S. Hawker</a> of Morwenstow wrote some poetry which was very popular in the Victorian period. The Scottish poet <a href="/wiki/W._S._Graham" title="W. S. Graham">W. S. Graham</a> lived in West Cornwall from 1944 until his death in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The poet <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Binyon" title="Laurence Binyon">Laurence Binyon</a> wrote "For the Fallen" (first published in 1914) while sitting on the cliffs between <a href="/wiki/Pentire_Point" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentire Point">Pentire Point</a> and The Rumps and a stone plaque was erected in 2001 to commemorate the fact. The plaque bears the inscription "FOR THE FALLEN / Composed on these cliffs, 1914". The plaque also bears below this the fourth stanza (sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Ode_of_Remembrance" title="Ode of Remembrance">"The Ode"</a>) of the poem:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><i>They shall grow not old, as we that are left grow old</i></dd>\n<dd><i>Age shall not weary them, nor the years condemn</i></dd>\n<dd><i>At the going down of the sun and in the morning</i></dd>\n<dd><i>We will remember them</i></dd></dl>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_literary_works">Other literary works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other literary works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Cornwall produced a substantial number of <a href="/wiki/Passion_play" class="mw-redirect" title="Passion play">passion plays</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Ordinalia" title="Ordinalia">Ordinalia</a> during the Middle Ages. Many are still extant, and provide valuable information about the Cornish language. See also <a href="/wiki/Cornish_literature" title="Cornish literature">Cornish literature</a>\n</p><p>Prolific writer <a href="/wiki/Colin_Wilson" title="Colin Wilson">Colin Wilson</a>, best known for his debut work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Outsider_(Colin_Wilson)" title="The Outsider (Colin Wilson)">The Outsider</a></i> (1956) and for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mind_Parasites" title="The Mind Parasites">The Mind Parasites</a></i> (1967), lived in <a href="/wiki/Gorran_Haven" title="Gorran Haven">Gorran Haven</a>, a small village on the southern Cornish coast. The writer <a href="/wiki/D._M._Thomas" title="D. M. Thomas">D. M. Thomas</a> was born in Redruth but lived and worked in Australia and the United States before returning to his native Cornwall. He has written novels, poetry, and other works, including translations from Russian.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy" title="Thomas Hardy">Thomas Hardy</a>\'s drama <i>The Queen of Cornwall</i> (1923) is a version of the Tristan story; the second act of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>\'s opera <i><a href="/wiki/Tristan_und_Isolde" title="Tristan und Isolde">Tristan und Isolde</a></i> takes place in Cornwall, as do <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Sullivan" title="Gilbert and Sullivan">Gilbert and Sullivan</a>\'s operettas <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pirates_of_Penzance" title="The Pirates of Penzance">The Pirates of Penzance</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ruddigore" title="Ruddigore">Ruddigore</a></i>. A level of <i><a href="/wiki/Tomb_Raider:_Legend" title="Tomb Raider: Legend">Tomb Raider: Legend</a></i>, a game dealing with Arthurian Legend, takes place in Cornwall at a museum above King Arthur\'s tomb.\n</p><p>The fairy tale <a href="/wiki/Jack_the_Giant_Killer" title="Jack the Giant Killer">Jack the Giant Killer</a> takes place in Cornwall.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Cornwall" title="Sport in Cornwall">Sport in Cornwall</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gerry_and_Ashley_Cawley.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Gerry_and_Ashley_Cawley.JPG/170px-Gerry_and_Ashley_Cawley.JPG" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Gerry_and_Ashley_Cawley.JPG/255px-Gerry_and_Ashley_Cawley.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Gerry_and_Ashley_Cawley.JPG/340px-Gerry_and_Ashley_Cawley.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1168" data-file-height="1760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gerry_and_Ashley_Cawley.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cornish_wrestling" title="Cornish wrestling">Cornish wrestling</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The main sports played in Cornwall are <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a>. Athletes from <a href="/wiki/Truro" title="Truro">Truro</a> have done well in <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a> fencing, winning several medals. <a href="/wiki/Surfing" title="Surfing">Surfing</a> is popular, particularly with tourists, thousands of which take to the water throughout the summer months. Some towns and villages have bowling clubs, and a wide variety of British sports are played throughout Cornwall. Cornwall is also one of the few <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Shinty_Club" title="Cornwall Shinty Club">places</a> in England where <a href="/wiki/Shinty" title="Shinty">shinty</a> is played; the <a href="/wiki/English_Shinty_Association" title="English Shinty Association">English Shinty Association</a> is based in <a href="/wiki/Penryn,_Cornwall" title="Penryn, Cornwall">Penryn</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_County_Cricket_Club" title="Cornwall County Cricket Club">Cornwall County Cricket Club</a> plays as one of the <a href="/wiki/Minor_counties_of_English_cricket" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor counties of English cricket">minor counties of English cricket</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Clegg10_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clegg10-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Truro, and all of the towns and some villages have <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> clubs belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_County_Football_Association" title="Cornwall County Football Association">Cornwall County Football Association</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rugby">Rugby</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornwall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Rugby">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rugby_in_Cornwall" class="mw-redirect" title="Rugby in Cornwall">Rugby in Cornwall</a></div>\n<p>Viewed as an "important identifier of ethnic affiliation", <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> has become a sport strongly tied to notions of Cornishness.<sup id="cite_ref-Harv221_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harv221-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and since the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union_in_Cornwall" title="Rugby union in Cornwall">rugby union</a> has emerged as one of the most popular spectator and team sports in Cornwall (perhaps the most popular), with professional Cornish rugby footballers being described as a "formidable force",<sup id="cite_ref-Clegg10_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clegg10-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> "naturally independent, both in thought and deed, yet paradoxically staunch English patriots whose top players have represented England with pride and passion".<sup id="cite_ref-CornishRug_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CornishRug-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1985, sports journalist <a href="/wiki/Alan_Gibson" title="Alan Gibson">Alan Gibson</a> made a direct connection between love of rugby in Cornwall and the ancient parish games of hurling and wrestling that existed for centuries before rugby officially began.<sup id="cite_ref-CornishRug_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CornishRug-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Among Cornwall\'s native sports are a distinctive form of Celtic wrestling related to <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Breton</a> wrestling, and <a href="/wiki/Hurling_the_Silver_Ball" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurling the Silver Ball">Cornish hurling</a>, a kind of mediaeval football played with a silver ball (distinct from <a href="/wiki/Hurling" title="Hurling">Irish Hurling</a>). <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Wrestling" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish Wrestling">Cornish Wrestling</a> is Cornwall\'s oldest sport and as Cornwall\'s native tradition it has travelled the world to places like <a href="/wiki/Victoria,_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria, Australia">Victoria, Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grass_Valley,_California" title="Grass Valley, California">Grass Valley, California</a> following the miners and <a href="/wiki/Gold_rushes" class="mw-redirect" title="Gold rushes">gold rushes</a>. Cornish hurling now takes place at <a href="/wiki/St._Columb_Major" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Columb Major">St. Columb Major</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Ives,_Cornwall" title="St Ives, Cornwall">St
